Aspect 6: Accumulation of Imperishable Entities
Sixth aspect: accumulation of imperishable entities shows finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the accumulation of rational souls (or any imperishable entities) over cycles demonstrates that past cycles must be finite.

Core Idea
If infinite cycles produced imperishable entities, we would have an infinite number today, but we can add to that number, contradicting infinity.

The sixth aspect uses the example of rational souls from the philosophers. If each cycle produces souls that never perish, and cycles were infinite, then the number of souls existing today would be infinite. Yet we can observe that the number at the time of the flood was less than now, proving increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the hypothetical: suppose in every past cycle something imperishable came into existence. If cycles were infinite, the total now would be infinite.
2
Understand the example
The philosophers believed rational souls are imperishable. So if cycles are infinite, the number of souls now would be infinite. But we can compare numbers across time: fewer at flood than now.
3
See the contradiction
An infinite number cannot be increased, but we know the number at flood is less than now, so the number has increased. Therefore, the number is not infinite, meaning cycles are finite.
4
Generalize the principle
Any set of imperishable entities produced over time would behave similarly, proving that the time series must be finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه. فالحركات الماضية متناهية . أما بيان المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : الحركات الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان . فتقريره من عشرة أوجه الأول : إنه كلما يدور زحل دورة واحدة ، فقد دارت الشمس ثلاثين دورة ، فوجب القطع بأن عدد دوران الشمس أكثر من عدد دوران زحل بثلاثين مرة الثاني : لا شك أن السنة الواحدة تكون إثني عشر شهراً ، والشهر الواحد يكون ثلاثين يوماً . فعدد الأيام أكثر من عدد الشهور ، وهو أكثر من عدد السنين . فيثبت : أن الأحوال الماضية قد دخل فيها الزيادة والنقصان الثالث : إنا إذا أخذنا اليوم مع ليلته شيئاً واحداً ، فلا شك أنه ينقسم إلى ليل ونهار ، فيكون عدد المجموع : نصف عدد مجموع الشهر والليالي وذلك هو المطلوب الرابع : إنه كلما حدث يوم ، فإنه تزاد الجملة الماضية بيوم واحد ، فكان المجموع الحاصل قبل هذا اليوم أنقص من المجموع الحاصل بعد حصول هذا اليوم. وهذه الزيادات والنقصانات حاصلة أبداً . الخامس : إنا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية من هذا اليوم الحاضر إلى الأزل ، جملة واحدة. وأخذناها من أول وقت الطوفان إلى الأزل جملة أخرى . فلا شك أن الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية ، بالمقدار الذي حصل من زمان الطوفان إلى هذا اليوم . فإذا أطبقنا هاتين الجملتين من الجانب الذي يلينا ، فإما أن يمتد من الجانب الأول أبداً من غير حصول التفاوت ، أو لابد وأن يظهر التفاوت. والأول يقتضي أن يكون الزائد مساوياً للناقص . وهو محال . والثاني يقتضي أن تكون إحدى الجملتين ناقصة عن الأخرى . السادس : إنا لو قدرنا أنه حدث في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية شيء ، وبقي بحيث لا يغنى البتة . فعلى هذا التقدير لو كانت الدورات الماضية غير متناهية ، لكان مجموع تلك الأشياء المجتمعة في زماننا عدداً غير متناهي ومثاله : أن عند الفلاسفة حدث في كل دورة من الأدوار الماضية أعداد من النفوس الناطقة . ثم قالوا : النفوس الناطقة لا تقبل العدم، فعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يقال : إن مجموع النفوس الناطقة الموجودة في هذا اليوم : عدد غير متناه . ثم لا شك أن هذا المجموع قابل للزيادة والنقصان . لأن من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن العدد الذي كان حاصلا في زمان الطوفان أقل من العدد الذي حصل الآن
The first proof: Past movements are capable of increase and decrease. And everything that is like that is finite. Thus past movements are finite. As for the explanation of the first premise—our statement 'Past movements are capable of increase and decrease'—its elaboration is from ten aspects. First: Whenever Saturn completes one orbit, the Sun completes thirty orbits, so it is necessarily concluded that the number of the Sun's orbits is greater than the number of Saturn's orbits by thirty times. Second: There is no doubt that one year consists of twelve months, and one month consists of thirty days. Thus the number of days is greater than the number of months, and it is greater than the number of years. So it is established that past states have entered into increase and decrease. Third: If we take the day with its night as one thing, there is no doubt that it divides into night and day, so the number of the total is half the number of the total of the month and nights, and that is what is sought. Fourth: Whenever a day occurs, the past total is increased by one day, so the total obtained before this day is less than the total obtained after the occurrence of this day. And these increases and decreases occur perpetually. Fifth: If we take the past events from this present day to eternity as one total, and take them from the first time of the flood to eternity as another total, there is no doubt that the first total exceeds the second total by the amount that occurred from the time of the flood to this day. Then if we align these two totals from the side facing us, either the first side extends forever without any difference occurring, or the difference must appear. The first would require that the excess be equal to the deficit, which is impossible. The second requires that one of the two totals be deficient with respect to the other. Sixth: If we suppose that in every one of the past cycles something occurred and remained such that it never perishes at all, then on this supposition, if the past cycles were infinite, the sum of those things gathered in our time would be an infinite number. An example of this: According to the philosophers, in every one of the past cycles numbers of rational souls occurred. Then they said: Rational souls do not admit non-existence. On this supposition, it necessarily follows that the sum of the rational souls existing in this day is an infinite number. Then there is no doubt that this sum is capable of increase and decrease, because it is known necessarily that the number that existed at the time of the flood is less than the number that exists now.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the first of ten proofs that a body cannot be moving from eternity. The proof argues that past movements are finite because they are capable of increase and decrease. The author first states the proof: past movements admit increase and decrease; anything that admits increase and decrease is finite; therefore past movements are finite. Then he elaborates the first premise—that past movements admit increase and decrease—through ten aspects, of which six are given here. The first aspect compares the orbits of Saturn and the Sun: Saturn's slower orbit means the Sun completes more orbits, showing a definite numerical relationship, so past orbits are countable and thus finite. The second aspect uses the subdivisions of time (years, months, days) to show that past time units are related by increase. The third aspect shows that a day-and-night period divides into two parts, so the total number of such periods is half the total number of parts, implying a finite partition. The fourth aspect notes that each new day adds to the past total, so the total before was less, proving increase and decrease. The fifth aspect compares two intervals: from now to eternity and from the flood to eternity; if both were infinite, aligning them would either imply no difference or a difference; the first leads to an impossible equality of excess and deficit, so the second must hold, meaning one total is less than the other, proving finitude. The sixth aspect uses a hypothetical accumulation of imperishable entities per cycle; if cycles were infinite, the accumulated number would be infinite; but since the number at the flood is less than now, the total is capable of increase and decrease, contradicting the possibility of an infinite actual number. Each aspect reinforces that past movements are not merely conceptually finite but demonstrably subject to increase and decrease, which entails finitude.

Key Terms
دورات (cycles)
النفوس الناطقة (rational souls)
عدم (non-existence/perishing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if you accept the philosophers' claim about rational souls.
Correction: The argument is conditional: if cycles were infinite, then the total would be infinite, but we observe increase, so cycles are finite. The example is illustrative.

Checkpoint
What follows if past cycles were infinite and each cycle produced an imperishable entity?
A) The number of such entities today would be finite.
B) The number would be infinite.
C) The number would be zero.
D) The number would decrease over time.

Key Takeaway
If cycles were infinite, imperishable entities would form an infinite set, but we observe increase, so cycles must be finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every cycle adds something imperishable, what are you contributing to the eternal accumulation? Does the finitude of your time on earth make you more intentional about what you leave behind?
?
The philosophers assumed an infinite past, but this argument shows it's impossible. How does the reality that your existence is not a product of an endless chain change your sense of purpose and accountability to the Creator?

Argument 7: Accumulation of Souls
Sanusi's seventh argument using soul accumulation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the thought experiment of souls accumulating from each past cycle shows that past cycles must be subject to increase and decrease.

Core Idea
If past cycles were infinite, the number of souls would be infinitely greater, proving cycles can change.

The seventh argument uses a simple supposition: if each infinite past cycle produced just one soul that remained, we would now have an infinite number of souls. But since each cycle actually produced many souls, the current number would be infinitely greater. This leads to the conclusion that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease, as they are less than the number of souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Allow the author's reasoning to become clear.
Pay attention to the conditional 'If we suppose'.
2
Identify the two cases
Notice the author compares a hypothetical (one soul per cycle) with the actual (many souls per cycle).
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease. Ask yourself: why does this follow from the comparison?
The reasoning implies that the number of cycles is less than the number of souls, so cycles can be increased or decreased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو قدرنا أنه حصل في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية ، الغير المتناهية : نفس واحدة . وبقيت . لكان قد حصل الآن أعداد لا نهاية لها من النفوس . فإذا علمنا : أن الحاصل في كل واحد من الأدوار الماضية أعداد كثيرة من النفوس ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العدد الموجود الآن من النفوس أضعافاً غير متناهية مراراً كثيرة. يدل على أن عدد الأدوار الماضية قابل للزيادة والنقصان . فإنا بينا أن عدد الأدوار الماضية، أقل من عدد النفوس الناطقة • والثامن : وهو أن عدد الأدوار الماضية. إما أن يكون شفعاً أو وتراً . فإن كان شفعاً فهو أنقص من الوتر ، الذي فوقه بواحد . وإن كان وتراً ، فهو أنقص من الشفع الذي فوقه بواحد . وعلى جميع الأحوال ، فالأمور الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان التاسع : إن الأدوار الماضية لها عدد فنصف ذلك العدد : أقل من كله فنصفه متناه، فضعفه : ضعف للمتناهي. وضعف المتناهي : متناهي. العاشر : لا شك أنه حصل لكل واحد من الأفلاك : أدوار مخصوصة على حدة . فلو كانت أدوار الفلك الأعظم غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك الثوابت ، غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك : زحل » غير متناهية . وكذا القول في البواقي . فنقول : لا شك أن عدد مجموع هذه الأدوار : أكثر من عدد أدوار الفلك الأعظم ، وحده . ومن عدد أدوار فلك : الثوابت ، وحده . وهذا يدل على أن عدد الأحوال الماضية يقبل الزيادة والنقصان
The seventh: If we suppose that in every cycle of the past infinite cycles there occurred one soul and it remained, then now there would have occurred numbers without end of souls. Since we know that in every one of the past cycles many souls occurred, then it necessarily follows that the number of souls now existing is infinitely many times greater. This indicates that the number of past cycles is subject to increase and decrease. For we have shown that the number of past cycles is less than the number of rational souls. The eighth: The number of past cycles is either even or odd. If it is even, it is less than the odd which is above it by one. If it is odd, it is less than the even which is above it by one. In all cases, past things are subject to increase and decrease. The ninth: Past cycles have a number, then half of that number is less than the whole; so half of it is finite. Its double is a double of a finite, and the double of a finite is finite. The tenth: There is no doubt that for each of the spheres there are particular cycles separately. So if the cycles of the greatest sphere are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of fixed stars are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of Saturn are infinite, and similarly for the rest, then we say: there is no doubt that the number of all these cycles together is greater than the number of cycles of the greatest sphere alone, and greater than the number of cycles of the sphere of fixed stars alone. This indicates that the number of past states accepts increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments (seventh through tenth) supporting the premise that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease. The seventh argument uses the accumulation of souls: if each infinite past cycle had one soul remaining, there would be infinitely many souls now; but since each cycle contained many souls, the current number would be infinitely greater, yet the number of past cycles is less than the number of souls, showing increase and decrease. The eighth argument uses parity: if the number of past cycles is even, it is less than the next odd; if odd, it is less than the next even; thus it can increase or decrease. The ninth argument: past cycles have a number; half of that number is finite, so its double (the whole) is finite, implying the number is finite and thus subject to increase/decrease. The tenth argument compares cycles of different spheres: the total of all past cycles exceeds the cycles of any single sphere, showing that the number of past states can be increased or decreased.

Key Terms
الأدوار الماضية
النفوس الناطقة
الزيادة والنقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite series cannot be compared in size.
Correction: The argument shows that if past cycles were infinite, the number of souls would be infinitely greater, which is absurd; therefore cycles must be finite and subject to increase.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh argument conclude about past cycles?
A) They are infinite.
B) They are finite and subject to increase and decrease.
C) They are equal to the number of souls.
D) They are unchanging.

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument uses a thought experiment to prove that past cycles cannot be infinite and are subject to increase and decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the thought experiment: if each past cycle produced only one soul, the number of souls would be infinite. But you know that many souls exist. How does this logical realization affect your certainty that this universe had a beginning? Are you truly convinced that the past is finite, or do you still entertain doubts? Commit to memorizing this proof so you can defend it.
?
The argument shows that an infinite past leads to contradictions. Your own existence is contingent on a finite chain of causes. Does this make you feel more dependent on the Creator? How often do you reflect on the fact that you were brought into being after not existing? Write down one action you will take today to remind yourself of your createdness.
?
Sanusi's proof uses a simple counting exercise to demonstrate a profound truth. Are you willing to engage your intellect in matters of faith, or do you rely solely on tradition? This lesson calls you to think deeply. What one intellectual effort will you make this week to strengthen your understanding of tawhid?

Argument 8: Even and Odd
Sanusi's eighth argument using parity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the parity (even/odd) of past cycles demonstrates that they are subject to increase and decrease.

Core Idea
If past cycles are even or odd, they can always be increased by one, showing they are not fixed.

The eighth argument is simple: the number of past cycles is either even or odd. If even, it is less than the next odd; if odd, it is less than the next even. In either case, the number can be increased by one, and by symmetry decreased, indicating that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two cases: even and odd.
The argument uses basic properties of integers.
2
Understand the reasoning
If the number is even, it is one less than the following odd. If odd, it is one less than the following even.
3
See the conclusion
Since it can be increased by one, it is subject to increase; by reversal, it can be decreased.
This shows the number is not absolute or infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو قدرنا أنه حصل في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية ، الغير المتناهية : نفس واحدة . وبقيت . لكان قد حصل الآن أعداد لا نهاية لها من النفوس . فإذا علمنا : أن الحاصل في كل واحد من الأدوار الماضية أعداد كثيرة من النفوس ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العدد الموجود الآن من النفوس أضعافاً غير متناهية مراراً كثيرة. يدل على أن عدد الأدوار الماضية قابل للزيادة والنقصان . فإنا بينا أن عدد الأدوار الماضية، أقل من عدد النفوس الناطقة • والثامن : وهو أن عدد الأدوار الماضية. إما أن يكون شفعاً أو وتراً . فإن كان شفعاً فهو أنقص من الوتر ، الذي فوقه بواحد . وإن كان وتراً ، فهو أنقص من الشفع الذي فوقه بواحد . وعلى جميع الأحوال ، فالأمور الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان التاسع : إن الأدوار الماضية لها عدد فنصف ذلك العدد : أقل من كله فنصفه متناه، فضعفه : ضعف للمتناهي. وضعف المتناهي : متناهي. العاشر : لا شك أنه حصل لكل واحد من الأفلاك : أدوار مخصوصة على حدة . فلو كانت أدوار الفلك الأعظم غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك الثوابت ، غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك : زحل » غير متناهية . وكذا القول في البواقي . فنقول : لا شك أن عدد مجموع هذه الأدوار : أكثر من عدد أدوار الفلك الأعظم ، وحده . ومن عدد أدوار فلك : الثوابت ، وحده . وهذا يدل على أن عدد الأحوال الماضية يقبل الزيادة والنقصان
The seventh: If we suppose that in every cycle of the past infinite cycles there occurred one soul and it remained, then now there would have occurred numbers without end of souls. Since we know that in every one of the past cycles many souls occurred, then it necessarily follows that the number of souls now existing is infinitely many times greater. This indicates that the number of past cycles is subject to increase and decrease. For we have shown that the number of past cycles is less than the number of rational souls. The eighth: The number of past cycles is either even or odd. If it is even, it is less than the odd which is above it by one. If it is odd, it is less than the even which is above it by one. In all cases, past things are subject to increase and decrease. The ninth: Past cycles have a number, then half of that number is less than the whole; so half of it is finite. Its double is a double of a finite, and the double of a finite is finite. The tenth: There is no doubt that for each of the spheres there are particular cycles separately. So if the cycles of the greatest sphere are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of fixed stars are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of Saturn are infinite, and similarly for the rest, then we say: there is no doubt that the number of all these cycles together is greater than the number of cycles of the greatest sphere alone, and greater than the number of cycles of the sphere of fixed stars alone. This indicates that the number of past states accepts increase and decrease.

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments (seventh through tenth) supporting the premise that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease. The seventh argument uses the accumulation of souls: if each infinite past cycle had one soul remaining, there would be infinitely many souls now; but since each cycle contained many souls, the current number would be infinitely greater, yet the number of past cycles is less than the number of souls, showing increase and decrease. ?

Argument 9: Half and Double
Sanusi's ninth argument using half and double.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how taking half of the number of past cycles shows it must be finite and thus subject to increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Half of the number of past cycles is finite, so the whole number is finite and can change.

The ninth argument considers the number of past cycles as a quantity. Half of that number is less than the whole and is finite. If the number itself were infinite, half would also be infinite, not finite. Therefore, the number of past cycles must be finite and consequently subject to increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key steps: half is less than whole, half is finite, so whole is finite.
2
Understand the logic
If the number of cycles is N, then half N is less than N. The author asserts half N is finite. Then the double of half N (which is N) is finite.
This relies on the premise that the double of a finite number is finite.
3
Grasp the implication
Since N is finite, it cannot be infinite; therefore it can be increased or decreased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : لو قدرنا أنه حصل في كل دورة من الدورات الماضية ، الغير المتناهية : نفس واحدة . وبقيت . لكان قد حصل الآن أعداد لا نهاية لها من النفوس . فإذا علمنا : أن الحاصل في كل واحد من الأدوار الماضية أعداد كثيرة من النفوس ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون العدد الموجود الآن من النفوس أضعافاً غير متناهية مراراً كثيرة. يدل على أن عدد الأدوار الماضية قابل للزيادة والنقصان . فإنا بينا أن عدد الأدوار الماضية، أقل من عدد النفوس الناطقة • والثامن : وهو أن عدد الأدوار الماضية. إما أن يكون شفعاً أو وتراً . فإن كان شفعاً فهو أنقص من الوتر ، الذي فوقه بواحد . وإن كان وتراً ، فهو أنقص من الشفع الذي فوقه بواحد . وعلى جميع الأحوال ، فالأمور الماضية قابلة للزيادة والنقصان التاسع : إن الأدوار الماضية لها عدد فنصف ذلك العدد : أقل من كله فنصفه متناه، فضعفه : ضعف للمتناهي. وضعف المتناهي : متناهي. العاشر : لا شك أنه حصل لكل واحد من الأفلاك : أدوار مخصوصة على حدة . فلو كانت أدوار الفلك الأعظم غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك الثوابت ، غير متناهية ، وكذلك أدوار فلك : زحل » غير متناهية . وكذا القول في البواقي . فنقول : لا شك أن عدد مجموع هذه الأدوار : أكثر من عدد أدوار الفلك الأعظم ، وحده . ومن عدد أدوار فلك : الثوابت ، وحده . وهذا يدل على أن عدد الأحوال الماضية يقبل الزيادة والنقصان
The seventh: If we suppose that in every cycle of the past infinite cycles there occurred one soul and it remained, then now there would have occurred numbers without end of souls. Since we know that in every one of the past cycles many souls occurred, then it necessarily follows that the number of souls now existing is infinitely many times greater. This indicates that the number of past cycles is subject to increase and decrease. For we have shown that the number of past cycles is less than the number of rational souls. The eighth: The number of past cycles is either even or odd. If it is even, it is less than the odd which is above it by one. If it is odd, it is less than the even which is above it by one. In all cases, past things are subject to increase and decrease. The ninth: Past cycles have a number, then half of that number is less than the whole; so half of it is finite. Its double is a double of a finite, and the double of a finite is finite. The tenth: There is no doubt that for each of the spheres there are particular cycles separately. So if the cycles of the greatest sphere are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of fixed stars are infinite, and likewise the cycles of the sphere of Saturn are infinite, and similarly for the rest, then we say: there is no doubt that the number of all these cycles together is greater than the number of cycles of the greatest sphere alone, and greater than the number of cycles of the sphere of fixed stars alone. This indicates that the number of past states accepts increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments (seventh through tenth) supporting the premise that past cycles are subject to increase and decrease. The seventh argument uses the accumulation of souls: if each infinite past cycle had one soul remaining, there would be infinitely many souls now; but since each cycle contained many souls, the current number would be infinitely greater, yet the number of past cycles is less than the number of souls, showing increase and decrease. The eighth argument uses parity: if the number of past cycles is even, it is less than the next odd; if odd, it is less than the next even; thus it can increase or decrease. The ninth argument: past cycles have a number; half of that number is finite, so its double (the whole) is finite, implying the number is finite and thus subject to increase/decrease. The tenth argument compares cycles of different spheres: the total of all past cycles exceeds the cycles of any single sphere, showing that the number of past states can be increased or decreased.

Key Terms
ضعف المتناهي
الزيادة والنقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the ninth argument conclude about the number of past cycles?
A) It is infinite.
B) It is half of a finite number.
C) It is finite.
D) It is equal to its double.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument shows that since half of the number of past cycles is finite, the whole number must be finite and thus subject to increase and decrease.

What specific change can you make today to align with the truth that time is not infinite?
?

?
Sanusi's reasoning relies on the principle that a part is less than the whole. In your own life, do you often act as if your time is unlimited? How can you use this logical truth to prioritize what truly matters for the hereafter?

Argument 10: Cycles of Different Spheres
Sanusi's tenth argument using spheres' cycles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how comparing cycles of different spheres proves that the total number of past cycles can be increased.

Core Idea
The total cycles of all spheres exceed cycles of any single sphere, showing the number of past states can increase.

The tenth argument considers cycles attributed to different spheres: the greatest sphere, sphere of fixed stars, Saturn, etc. If each sphere's cycles were infinite, the sum would be greater than any single infinite set, which is impossible. Therefore, the total number of past states is subject to increase and decrease.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the list of spheres: greatest, fixed stars, Saturn, etc.
2
Understand the comparison
If each sphere has infinite cycles, then the total is greater than the cycles of any one sphere.
This is a contradiction if all are infinite, so the cycles cannot be infinite.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore past cycles are finite and can be increased or decreased.

Second Premise: Increase and Decrease Imply Finitude
Logical proof of finitude from increase/decrease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that anything accepting increase and decrease must be finite, and how this establishes the finitude of past cycles.

Core Idea
Increase and decrease cannot occur in an infinite series.

This lesson unpacks the second premise of a larger argument about past cycles: that if something can be increased or decreased, it must be finite. The passage provides a tight logical proof: both the lesser and the greater in a comparative relation are shown to be finite. Understanding this reasoning is essential for appreciating why theologians conclude that time itself cannot be infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow settle before dissecting it.
The passage is dense but concise; take your time.
2
Identify the two components: deficient and greater
The argument first focuses on the deficient (nāqiṣ) number, then on the greater (zā’id) number. Notice how each is defined and why each must be finite.
The deficient number is defined as reaching a point where nothing remains.
3
Trace the logical deduction
Follow the steps: 1) The deficient number has an endpoint → it is finite. 2) The greater exceeds the deficient by a finite amount → it too is finite.
The reasoning relies on the principle that adding a finite to a finite yields a finite.
4
Apply to past cycles
The conclusion applies the premise to past states and cycles, which were shown to admit increase and decrease, therefore they must be finite.
This completes the second premise of the overall argument.
5
Reflect on a common misunderstanding
Consider why an infinite series cannot be increased or decreased. This argument shows that any series that admits comparison of greater and lesser is necessarily finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : أن كل ما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان فله عدد متناه . فتقريره : أن الشيء إنما يكون أنقص من غيره ، لو انتهى ذلك الشيء إلى حيث لا يبقى منه شيء. مع أنه بقي من ذلك الزائد شيء ، وكل ما انتهى عدده إلى حيث لا يبقى منه شيء ، كان متناهياً ، وينتج : أن كل عدد ناقص فهو متناه . ثم إن الزائد إنما زاد على ذلك الناقص المتناهي : بعدد متناه . والزائد على العدد المتناهي ، بعدد متناهي يجب أن يكون متناهياً . فهذا الزائد أيضاً يجب أن يكون متناهياً. فالأحوال الماضية، والأدوار الماضية : يجب أن تكون متناهية. هذا تمام تقرير هذه الحجة [ والله أعلم ]
As for the second premise: it is our statement that everything that accepts increase and decrease has a finite number. Its explanation: a thing is less than another only if it reaches a point where nothing of it remains, while something of the greater remains. And everything whose number reaches a point where nothing of it remains is finite. Thus it follows that every deficient number is finite. Then the greater is only greater than that finite deficient by a finite number. And what is greater than a finite number by a finite number must be finite. So this greater must also be finite. Therefore, past states and past cycles must be finite. This is the complete formulation of this argument. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage provides the second premise of the overall argument: that anything accepting increase and decrease is finite. The explanation follows a logical chain: a deficient (lesser) number must have an endpoint where nothing remains, so it is finite. The greater number is only greater by a finite amount, and adding a finite amount to a finite number yields a finite number. Hence, both the deficient and the greater are finite. Applying this to past cycles, which were shown to be subject to increase and decrease, they must be finite. The passage concludes the argument with 'And Allah knows best.'

Key Terms

الزيادة والنقصان
العدد المتناهي
الناقص
الزائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite series can be increased (e.g., adding more elements), but this argument shows that if a series admits comparison of greater and lesser, it must be finite; an actual infinite cannot be increased or decreased.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that whatever is subject to increase or decrease must be finite, so an actual infinite cannot have such properties.

Checkpoint

A) It is infinite.
B) It is finite.
C) It is equal to the greater.
D) It has no number.

Key Takeaway
Anything that can be increased or decreased must be finite; this logical principle forces past cycles to be finite.

?
The second premise shows that past cycles had a beginning. Your own past has a beginning too—your birth. Does that fact make you feel accountable to the One who started it all? What will you do today to acknowledge that beginning?

Objection and Reply on Celestial Motion's Countability
Objection that celestial motion is indivisible and reply that it only applies without beginning/end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that celestial motion has no countable parts because it is a single continuous whole with no beginning or end, and how this challenges arguments based on numerical cycles.

Core Idea
Continuous motion without beginning or end has no countable parts.

This passage presents a critical objection against arguments that rely on counting cycles of celestial motion. The objector argues that celestial motion is a single continuous whole, not composed of discrete units, because it has no beginning or end. Understanding this objection and its underlying premise about rest is essential for evaluating proofs about the creation of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening line and note that the objector denies that celestial motion has numbers and parts. This is the core challenge.
The objector says the proof's basis is 'prohibited' (mamnūʿ).
2
Examine the premise about motion
The objector states that when a body moves from start to end, the motion is a single unified whole. It cannot be composed of discrete moments or parts without implying indivisible atoms.
This premise is supported by the rejection of atomism (jawhar fard).
3
Understand the role of rest
Observed motions have a first and last because they begin from rest and end in rest. Without rest, motion would have no beginning or end, making it one continuous thing.
This is key for the application to celestial motion.
4
Apply to celestial motion
Since celestial motion is believed to have no beginning, it has no first or last. Therefore, it is a single continuous motion without parts or numbers, undermining the previous proof.
The objector concludes that all arguments based on counting cycles collapse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : مدار هذا الدليل على أن الحركة الفلكية لها أعداد وأجزاء وذلك ممنوع . وهذا السؤال لا يتم إلا بتقرير مقدمة : وهي أنا نقول : الجسم إذا تحرك من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، فتلك الحركة: حركة واحدة في نفسها ، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إنها مركبة من أنات متلاصقة ، وأجزاء متعاقبة. إذ لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكانت تلك المسافة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ، وذلك باطل بالدلائل الدالة على نفي الجوهر الفرد . فيثبت : أن الحركة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها : حركة واحدة في الحقيقة ، وليست مركبة من أقسام وأجزاء. إذا عرفت هذا ، فنقول : إنما حصل الأول والآخر لهذه الحركات التي نشاهدها في عالمنا هذا : لأن ذلك المتحرك ابتدأ بالحركة بعد أن كان ساكناً ، وانتهى في آخر الأمر إلى السكون . ولو فرضنا عدم هذا السكون في أول هذه الحركة وآخرها ، لم يحصل لهذه الحركة أول ولا آخر بل كانت حركة واحدة في ذاتها، مبرأة عن الكثرة والعدد . وإذا عرفت هذا، فنقول : الحركة الفلكية ليس لها أول وآخر على مذهب القائلين بأنه لا أول لها ، فكانت في نفسها حركة واحدة متصلة أزلاً وأبداً . ولا يكون لها شيء من الأجزاء والأعداد . وجميع ما ذكرتم في كون تلك الدورات قابلة للزيادة والنقصان بحسب الأعداد، ومبني على ثبوت العدد ، فلما بطل ذلك سقط جميع ما ذكرتم .
For it is said: The basis of this argument is that celestial motion has numbers and parts, and that is prohibited. And this objection is only complete with the establishment of a premise: which is that we say: When a body moves from the beginning of the distance to its end, that motion is a single motion in itself, and it cannot be said that it is composed of contiguous moments and successive parts. For if it were so, that distance would be composed of indivisible parts, and that is false by the proofs indicating the negation of the individual substance. So it is established that motion from the beginning of the distance to its end is one motion in reality, and is not composed of sections and parts. If you know this, then we say: The first and last only occur for these motions we observe in our world because that moving object began motion after being at rest and ended in the last state to rest. And if we suppose the absence of this rest at the beginning of this motion and its end, then no first or last would occur for this motion; rather it would be a single motion in itself, free from multiplicity and number. And if you know this, then we say: Celestial motion has no first or last according to the view of those who say it has no beginning, so it is in itself a single continuous motion eternally and forever. And it has no parts or numbers. And all that you mentioned regarding those cycles being subject to increase and decrease in terms of numbers is based on the affirmation of number; so when that is nullified, all that you mentioned collapses.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection (فإن قيل) against the previous proof. The objector states that the proof depends on the assumption that celestial motion (al-ḥarakah al-falakiyyah) has countable numbers and parts (aʿdād wa ajzāʾ), but he denies this (mamnūʿ). To make the objection precise, he establishes a premise: when a body moves from start to finish, the motion is a single continuous whole, not composed of discrete moments (ānāt mutalāṣiqah) or successive parts (ajzāʾ mutaʿāqibah), because that would imply indivisible parts (ajzāʾ lā tatajazzā), which are refuted by proofs against the atom (jawhar fard). Hence motion is one in reality. Then he applies this: observed motions have beginning and end because they start from rest and end in rest; but if rest were absent, motion would have no beginning or end. Celestial motion, on the view that it has no beginning, has no first or last, and is thus a single continuous motion without parts or numbers. Therefore, the claim that the cycles are subject to numerical increase or decrease is baseless because it presupposes countable units. The objector concludes that the previous proof collapses.

Key Terms
الحركة الفلكية
أعداد وأجزاء
أنات متلاصقة
أجزاء متعاقبة
الجوهر الفرد
أول وآخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that celestial motion has numbers because it is infinite.
Correction: The objection is that celestial motion is a single continuous whole due to absence of rest at boundaries, not because of infinity per se. The proof presupposes divisibility, which is what is denied.

Checkpoint

A) Celestial motion is imperceptible.
B) Motion from start to finish is a single continuous whole, not composed of discrete units.
C) The ancient philosophers denied numbers.
D) The cycles are infinite.

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that celestial motion, being eternal without beginning or end, is a single continuous motion with no countable parts, thereby invalidating proofs that rely on numerical cycles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life has a clear beginning (birth) and end (death), making every action countable. If you knew that every single deed—even the smallest—is recorded and will be accounted for, how would you change the way you spend your moments today?
?
The objector argues that without rest at the boundaries, motion has no first or last and is uncountable. In your spiritual life, the moments of repentance and renewal act as 'rests' that break the continuity of sin. Have you placed enough such 'rests' in your life so that your sins are not an unending, unbroken chain? What is one specific step you will take this week to create a break in a sinful pattern?

The Sum of Past Cycles Cannot Increase or Decrease
Concedes cycles, then refutes that their total can increase via two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments that refute the idea that the total sum of an infinite series of past cycles can be subject to increase or decrease, even if we grant the existence of cycles for the sake of argument.

Core Idea
An infinite sum has no existence, so it cannot increase or decrease.

Having conceded the possibility of cycles for the sake of argument, the text now attacks the notion that the total sum of all past cycles could be increased or decreased. This is the crucial move: even if each cycle is finite, the infinite sum as a whole has no reality, and therefore cannot be subject to change. Two arguments are given, one based on the definition of 'sum' and the other on the non-existence of the infinite aggregate.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two possible subjects
Read the opening where the author distinguishes between each individual cycle and the total sum. Understand why the first is conceded.
The first option (each cycle) is finite, so no dispute. The problem is the second option.
2
Understand the first argument (circularity)
The first argument says that calling something a 'sum' implies it is finite. But to claim the sum of past cycles is finite would assume what is being proved, leading to circularity.
This argument shows that the opponent's premise (that the sum can increase/decrease) forces them to assume finitude.
3
Understand the second argument (non-existence)
The second and stronger argument: the sum of past cycles has no existence at all—not externally (only one cycle exists at a time) nor in the mind (the mind cannot grasp infinite detail). What does not exist cannot be subject to increase or decrease.
This argument is independent of the circularity point.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutes the opponent's claim that an infinite regress of past cycles leads to a present effect. The total sum is not a real entity.
This argument is crucial in the broader debate on the finitude of past time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : حصول الأعداد والدورات، لكنا نقول : المحكوم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان : إما كل واحد من تلك الدورات ، أو مجموعها . والأول لا نزاع فيه . لأن غاية الكلام فيه : أنه يلزم كون كل واحد من تلك الأدوار متناهياً ، وذلك لا نزاع فيه . وأما الثاني : فهو باطل من وجهين : الأول : إن الشيء لا يوصف بكونه مجموعاً وجملة ، إلا إذا كان متناهياً . فنقول : مجموع الأدوار الماضية كذا وكذا . أو قولنا : جملة الأمور الماضية كذا وكذا : إنما يصح لو كانت تلك الأمور الماضية متناهية، وحينئذ يتوقف صحة المقدمة على صحة المطلوب ، وذلك يوجب الدور . والثاني : مجموع الأدوار الماضية لا وجود له البتة وما لا وجود له البتة امتنع الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان . إنما قلنا : إن مجموع الأمور الماضية ، لا وجود له البتة ، لأنه لو حصل لهذا المجموع : وجود . لكان إما أن يكون موجوداً في الأعيان ، أو في الأذهان. والأول باطل . لأن مجموع الأدوار الماضية لم يحصل البتة في شيء من الأوقات الماضية والحاضرة والمستقبله . بل الموجود فيه أبداً دورة واحدة فقط . والثاني أيضاً باطل . لأن الذهن لا يقوى على استحضار أمور لا نهاية لها على التفصيل . وإن كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يحضر في الذهن صور الأدوار التي لا نهاية لها على التفصيل . فيثبت : أن مجموع الأمور الماضية لا وجود له البتة ، لا في الأعيان ولا في الأذهان. وإنما قلنا : إن كل ما كان كذلك ، امتنع الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان . وذلك لأن كون الشيء قابلاً لشيء آخر : فرع على كون ذلك القابل موجوداً في نفسه لأن حصول شيء لشيء آخر له ، فرع على حصوله في نفسه فإذا لم يكن لهذا المجموع حصول البتة ، استحال على أن
We grant the occurrence of numbers and cycles, but we say: That which is judged to be subject to increase and decrease is either each one of those cycles, or their total. The first is undisputed, because the ultimate point in it is that it entails that each one of those cycles is finite, and that is undisputed. As for the second, it is false in two ways: The first: A thing is not described as being a sum or a whole unless it is finite. So we say: The sum of past cycles is such and such, or our saying: The totality of past things is such and such, is only valid if those past things were finite, and then the validity of the premise depends on the validity of the conclusion, which leads to circularity. The second: The sum of past cycles has no existence at all, and what has no existence at all is prevented from being judged to be subject to increase and decrease. We say that the sum of past things has no existence at all, because if this sum had existence, it would either exist in external objects (aʿyān) or in minds (adhhān). The first is false, because the sum of past cycles has never occurred at any past, present, or future time. Rather, what exists in it is always only one cycle. The second is also false, because the mind is not capable of summoning things that are infinite in detail. And if that is so, it is impossible for the images of cycles that are infinite in detail to be present in the mind. So it is established that the sum of past things has no existence at all, neither in external objects nor in minds. And we say that everything that is like that, it is impossible to judge it to be subject to increase and decrease. That is because a thing being receptive to something else is subsidiary to that receiver existing in itself, because the obtaining of something for another thing is subsidiary to its obtaining in itself. So when this sum has no obtaining at all, it is impossible for it to be ...
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents the second part of the response, after conceding (sallamnā) for the sake of argument that cycles exist. The author distinguishes between two possible subjects of increase/decrease: (1) each individual cycle, and (2) the total sum of all cycles. The first is uncontroversially finite, so no problem. The second is rejected by two arguments. First argument: something is only called a 'sum' or 'totality' if it is finite; but to claim the sum of past cycles is finite requires assuming what is to be proved (the finitude of the series), leading to circularity (dawr). Second argument: the sum of past cycles has no existence at all (lā wujūd lahu al-battah). It cannot exist externally because only one cycle exists at any time; it cannot exist in the mind because the mind cannot comprehend an infinite series in detail. Since the sum does not exist, it cannot be subject to increase or decrease, as a thing must exist to receive a property. The passage ends mid-sentence on the bottom of page 254, continuing the same thought.

Key Terms
مجموع الأدوار الماضية
زيادة ونقصان
دور
الذهن
الأعيان
الأذهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that each cycle is finite, or it denies the existence of cycles altogether.
Correction: The argument explicitly accepts that each cycle is finite and concedes cycles for argument; it only attacks the reality of the infinite sum.

Checkpoint
Why does the second argument claim that the sum of past cycles cannot be subject to increase or decrease?
A) Because the sum is infinite by definition.
B) Because the sum has no existence at all.
C) Because each cycle is infinite.
D) Because the mind can comprehend the sum easily.

Key Takeaway
The infinite sum of past cycles has no existence whatsoever, so it cannot be increased or decreased.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You spend so much time dwelling on the sum of your past mistakes, as if they exist as a weight that grows heavier. But this lesson teaches that the total of past moments has no existence at all. Will you let go of the illusion of an ever-increasing burden and instead face only the present moment, where real change is possible?
?
If the infinite sum of past cycles does not exist, then what about your endless worries about the future? Are you trying to grasp a totality that does not exist? What would it mean for you to trust Allah with the sum of your days and focus only on the single cycle of action that is before you now?
?
You believe your past deeds—good and bad—accumulate into a total that defines you. But if that total has no reality, what does that say about your need to define yourself by your past? Can you see yourself as free to act anew, each cycle independent, rather than weighed down by a non-existent sum?

The Circularity Objection to Increase/Decrease of Past Cycles
First refutation: circularity in calling the sum finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first refutation against increase/decrease in the sum of past cycles: that labeling it as a 'sum' presupposes finiteness, leading to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Calling something a 'sum' assumes it is finite — assuming that is circular.

The author first distinguishes whether increase/decrease applies to each individual cycle or the entire sum. He quickly dismisses the first as uncontroversial. Then he turns to the sum and presents two objections. In this lesson, we examine the first objection: to speak of a 'sum' of past cycles is only valid if the cycles are finite, but that is exactly what needs to be proven, resulting in circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author sets up the distinction between individual cycles and their sum.
The individual cycles being finite is not disputed.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage says increase/decrease could apply to each cycle or to the sum. The first is uncontested; the second is the focus.
3
Grasp the circularity argument
The first refutation argues that describing something as a sum or total presupposes it is finite. So if we say 'the sum of past cycles is such and such', we are already assuming the cycles are finite, which is what we want to prove. This makes the argument circular.
Circularity (دور) occurs when a premise depends on the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزيادة والنقصان : إما كل واحد من تلك الدورات ، أو مجموعها . والأول لا نزاع فيه . لأن غاية الكلام فيه : أنه يلزم كون كل واحد من تلك الأدوار متناهياً ، وذلك لا نزاع فيه . وأما الثاني : فهو باطل من وجهين : الأول : إن الشيء لا يوصف بكونه مجموعاً وجملة ، إلا إذا كان متناهياً . فنقول : مجموع الأدوار (۱) الماضية كذا وكذا . أو قولنا : جملة الأمور الماضية كذا وكذا : إنما يصح لو كانت تلك الأمور الماضية متناهية، وحينئذ يتوقف صحة المقدمة على صحة المطلوب ، وذلك يوجب الدور . والثاني : مجموع الأدوار الماضية لا وجود له البتة [ وما لا وجود لـه البتة (٢) ] امتنع الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان . إنما قلنا : إن مجموع الأمور الماضية ، لا وجود له البتة ، لأنه لو حصل لهذا المجموع : وجود . لكان إما أن يكون موجوداً في الأعيان ، أو في الأذهان. والأول باطل . لأن مجموع الأدوار الماضية لم يحصل البتة في شيء من الأوقات الماضية والحاضرة والمستقبله . بل الموجود فيه أبداً دورة واحدة فقط . والثاني أيضاً باطل . لأن الذهن لا يقوى على استحضار (۳) أمور لا نهاية لها على التفصيل . وإن كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يحضر [ في الذهن (۹) ] صور الأدوار التي لا نهاية لها على التفصيل . فيثبت : أن مجموع الأمور الماضية لا وجود له البتة ، لا في الأعيان ولا في الأذهان. وإنما قلنا : إن كل ما كان كذلك ، امتنع الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان . وذلك لأن كون الشيء قابلاً لشيء آخر : فرع على كون ذلك القابل موجوداً في نفسه [ لأن حصول شيء لشيء آخر له ، فرع على حصوله في نفسه (٥) ] فإذا لم يكن لهذا المجموع حصول البتة ، استحال على أن يحصل له غيره ، فامتنع كونه قابلا للزيادة والنقصان . وهذا هو السؤال الذي عليه اعتماد الشيخ الرئيس [أبي (١) ] علي ابن سينا .
Increase and decrease: either each one of those cycles, or their sum. The first is uncontested, because the extent of the discussion therein is that it implies that each one of those cycles is finite, and that is uncontested. As for the second, it is invalid from two aspects: The first: A thing is not described as being a total and a whole unless it is finite. So we say: 'The sum of (such and such) past cycles,' or our statement: 'The sum of such and such past matters,' is only valid if those past matters were finite, and then the validity of the premise depends on the validity of the desired conclusion, and that necessitates circularity. The second: The sum of past cycles has no existence at all, and that which has no existence at all is prevented from being judged as subject to increase and decrease. We only say that the sum of past matters has no existence at all, because if this sum had existence, it would either exist in external entities (al-a'yan) or in minds (al-adhhan). The first is invalid, because the sum of past cycles has never occurred in any past, present, or future time. Rather, only one cycle ever exists at a time. The second is also invalid, because the mind is not capable of summoning things without end in detail. And if that is so, it is impossible for the forms of cycles without end to be present in the mind in detail. Thus it is established that the sum of past matters has no existence at all, neither in external entities nor in minds. And we only say that everything that is such is prevented from being judged as subject to increase and decrease, because a thing's being receptive of something else is conditional upon that receptacle existing in itself [because the occurrence of something to something else is conditional upon its occurrence in itself]. So if this sum has no occurrence at all, it is impossible for something else to occur to it, so it is prevented from being receptive to increase and decrease. And this is the objection upon which the Chief Master [Abu] 'Ali ibn Sina relies.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents two refutations of the claim that the sum of past cycles can be subject to increase or decrease (al-ziyada wa al-nuqsan). The author first distinguishes two possibilities: either each individual cycle is subject to increase/decrease, or the sum of all cycles is. The first is uncontroversial because it merely implies each cycle is finite. The second is the focus. Two arguments are given against it. The first argument: describing something as a 'sum' or 'total' presupposes that it is finite, so to speak of a 'sum of past cycles' already assumes finiteness, making the proof circular. The second argument: the sum of past cycles has no existence whatsoever. The author proves this by considering two types of existence: external (al-a'yan) and mental (al-adhhan). Externally, only one cycle exists at any given moment; the total sum never exists as a whole. Mentally, the mind cannot summon an infinite number of details, so the sum cannot be present in the mind. Therefore, the sum has no reality. Since something must exist to be receptive to increase/decrease, and this sum does not exist, it cannot be subject to increase/decrease. The passage ends by noting that this is the argument relied upon by Avicenna (Ibn Sina).

Key Terms
الزيادة والنقصان (increase and decrease)
مجموع الأدوار (sum of cycles)
متناهٍ (finite)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first refutation is about the cycles themselves being finite or not.
Correction: The first refutation is about the sum of cycles, not the cycles individually. The issue is that calling it a 'sum' already assumes finiteness, leading to circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the circularity in the first refutation?
A) It assumes the sum exists.
B) It assumes the sum is finite to prove it is finite.
C) It confuses cycles with numbers.
D) It relies on infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The first refutation shows that speaking of a 'sum of past cycles' already assumes finiteness, making the argument circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own faith, are there beliefs you hold that might be supported by circular reasoning—where your evidence already assumes what you are trying to prove? Take a moment today to identify one such belief and find an independent proof for it.
?
The passage shows that using the word 'sum' smuggles in an assumption of finiteness. What labels or phrases do you use in your daily life that might unwittingly assume something you haven't actually proven? Commit to questioning one such phrase today.
?
This lesson demonstrates the importance of logical precision in theological arguments. Are you content with surface-level understanding of your aqeedah, or do you strive to examine the logical foundations deeply? Write down one topic in Islamic creed you will study more rigorously this week.

The Second Refutation: The Sum of Past Cycles Has No Existence
Second refutation: sum of past cycles lacks existence externally and mentally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second refutation: the sum of past cycles has no existence in external reality or in the mind, so it cannot be subject to increase or decrease.

Core Idea
If something does not exist at all, it cannot receive increase or decrease.

Having dispatched the first objection, the author now presents a more fundamental argument: the sum of past cycles simply does not exist. He examines two possible modes of existence—external and mental—and shows that neither can contain the infinite sum. This argument, which the author attributes to Ibn Sina, decisively blocks the possibility of increase or decrease applying to the sum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the claim that the sum has no existence, followed by the proof through two possibilities.
The author uses a disjunction: existence is either external or mental.
2
Understand the claim of non-existence
The author claims the sum of past cycles has no existence at all (la wujud lahu al-batta). This is the core premise.
3
Examine the proof for external existence
If the sum existed externally, it would have to be present at some point in time. But only one cycle ever exists at a time; the total sum of all past cycles never coexists. So external existence is impossible.
4
Examine the proof for mental existence
If the sum existed in the mind, the mind would have to grasp all infinite details simultaneously, which is impossible. Therefore, mental existence is also impossible.
5
Connect to the conclusion
Since the sum has no existence in either realm, it is non-existent. A non-existent thing cannot be receptive to increase or decrease, because receptivity requires existence.
This is the argument upon which Ibn Sina relied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You constantly review the 'total' of your past sins and good deeds. But this passage argues that sum has no real existence. If that total does not exist, what is the weight you are carrying? Can you now repent without measuring your past as a burden?
?
The mind cannot grasp infinite details. Yet you may dwell on the endless minutiae of your past mistakes. How does this truth free you from the exhausting task of mentally cataloging your past? Will you choose to stop trying to hold a non-existent sum?
?

What would change if you anchored your self-worth not in a sum of past actions but in the present moment before your Creator?

Objection: Why Must Increase/Decrease Imply Finiteness?
First objection: challenging finiteness from increase/decrease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's challenge to the assumption that any sum subject to increase and decrease must be finite.

Core Idea
Increase/decrease does not automatically prove finiteness; an infinite sum could also increase.

The opponent grants that the sum can be judged as accepting increase and decrease, but questions why that entails finiteness. He proposes that perhaps each series goes on infinitely, one containing what the other lacks, and asks for the proof against this possibility. This lesson examines that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the concession
Read the opponent's opening concession: 'We concede: that sum can be judged as subject to increase and decrease.' Notice that he does not reject the premise.
The word 'sallamna' indicates a hypothetical concession.
2
Identify the challenge
The opponent then asks two questions: (1) Why does increase/decrease imply finiteness? (2) Why can't both series be infinite, one containing what the other lacks? Identify these challenges.
3
Reflect on the demand for proof
The opponent ends by asking for the proof that his alternative is impossible. Consider what kind of proof would be needed to rule out an infinite series that increases.
This sets up the need for a demonstration.

سلمنا : أن ذلك المجموع يصح الحكم عليه بقبول الزيادة والنقصان فلم قلتم : إن كل ما كان كذلك ، فهو متناه ؟ ولم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن كل واحد منها (٢) يذهب إلى غير النهاية ، ولا ينتهي واحد منهما إلى الانقطاع ، مع أنه يكون أحدهما مشتملا على ما لم يحصل في الآخر ؟ وما الدليل على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ سلمنا (۳) : أن ما ذكرتموه ، يدل على أنه يمتنع وجود ما لا نهاية له ، إلا أنا نقول : ما ذكرتم ينتقض بأمور : الأول : إنكم تقولون : إنه تعالى عالم بما لا نهاية له من المعلومات. ولا شك أن الإضافة إلى أحد تلك المعلومات ، مغايرة إلى المعلوم الآخر ، لأن كون العالم عالم بذلك المعلوم المعين ، إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين ذلك المعلوم . وكون ذلك العالم [ عالماً (1)] بمعلوم آخر إضافة أخرى بين ذلك العالم وبين المعلوم الآخر . والدليل على تغاير هذه الإضافات : أنه يصح أن يعلم كون العالم عالماً بهذا المعلوم ، مع الشك في كون ذلك العالم عالماً بالمعلوم الآخر . والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم. فيثبت : أن كون العالم عالماً بهذا المعلوم : مغاير لكونه عالماً بذلك المعلوم الآخر . وإذا ثبت هذا فكونه تعالى عالماً بمعلومات لا نهاية لها : يقتضي حصول إضافات لا نهاية لها في ذاته وذلك [ نقض (٥) ] صريح على قولكم : إن أعداد لا نهاية لها : محال . لا يقال : هذه الإضافات أمور لا حصول لها وجود في الأعيان ، فزال الإشكال. لأنا نقول : نحن لا نريد بهذه الإضافة إلا كونه
We concede: that sum can be judged as subject to increase and decrease. So why do you say that everything that is such is finite? And why is it not permissible to say that each one of them goes on infinitely, and neither of them ends in termination, while one of them contains what did not occur in the other? And what is the proof that it is not permissible that the matter be such? We concede: that what you have mentioned indicates that it is impossible for something without end to exist, except that we say: what you have mentioned is contradicted by certain matters: The first: You say that He, exalted, is knowing (alim) of an infinite number of objects of knowledge (ma'lumat). There is no doubt that the relation (idafa) to one of those objects of knowledge is different from the relation to another object of knowledge, because the world's being knowing of that specific object of knowledge is a specific relation between the knower and that object. And that knower's being knowing of another object of knowledge is another relation between that knower and the other object. The proof of the diversity of these relations is that it is possible to know that the knower is knowing of this object while doubting whether that knower is knowing of the other object. And the known is different from the not-known. Thus it is established that the knower's being knowing of this object is different from his being knowing of that other object. And when this is established, His being knowing of infinite objects of knowledge necessitates the occurrence of infinite relations in His essence, and that is a clear contradiction of your statement that infinite numbers are impossible. It cannot be said: These relations are things that have no existence in external entities, so the problem disappears. Because we say: We do not intend by this relation except His being...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage contains two responses from the opponent, each introduced with 'sallimna' (we concede). The first concession: the opponent grants that the sum can be subject to increase/decrease, but then challenges the claim that this necessarily implies finiteness. He asks why it cannot be that both series go on infinitely without end, one containing what the other lacks. The second concession: the opponent grants that the proof shows the impossibility of an infinite regress, but then raises a counterexample from divine attributes. Specifically, God is knowing of an infinite number of objects of knowledge. Each object of knowledge entails a distinct relation (idafa) between the knower and the known. Since these relations are distinct (as proven by the fact that one can know God knows X while doubting He knows Y), and since God knows infinite objects, there are infinite relations in God's essence. This contradicts the claim that infinite numbers are impossible. The opponent anticipates an objection: these relations have no external existence. He replies that he does not mean the relation as something separate; it is simply God's being knowing, which is real. The passage cuts off here.

Key Terms
سلمنا (we concede)
متناهٍ (finite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the opponent concede at the beginning of the first objection?
A) That the sum is finite.
B) That the sum can be judged as subject to increase and decrease.
C) That infinite series are impossible.
D) That God's knowledge is infinite.

Key Takeaway
The opponent questions the link between increase/decrease and finiteness, suggesting that an infinite sum might still increase.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah is eternal (qadīm), have you ever rationally traced the implications of that belief? The opponent here demands a proof for why increase implies finiteness. Are you confident you could defend the logical foundation of Allah's eternality if someone questioned it? What will you do to strengthen your understanding?
?
The opponent's method begins with a concession ('sallamna') — he acknowledges part of the argument before challenging the conclusion. In your own discussions about faith, do you first find common ground with the other person? How can you apply this prophetic wisdom to become more effective in conveying the truth?
?
This lesson reveals that even a basic assumption like 'increase implies finiteness' requires rigorous proof before being accepted in matters of creed. What are some beliefs you hold that you have never critically examined? Commit to studying one of them with the same intellectual honesty the opponent demands.

Objection: God's Infinite Knowledge Implies Infinite Relations
Second objection: counterexample from God's infinite knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that God's knowledge of infinite objects necessitates infinite relations in His essence, contradicting the claim that infinite numbers are impossible.

Core Idea
Infinite objects of knowledge entail infinite distinct relations in God.

The opponent concedes the proof shows impossibility of infinite regress, but raises a counterexample: God knows an infinite number of objects of knowledge, each requiring a distinct relation (idafa) between God and that object. This seems to entail infinite relations in God's essence, which would contradict the previous conclusion. This lesson examines that argument and the opponent's anticipation of a reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the second concession
The opponent again says 'sallamna' – we concede that the proof shows impossibility of infinite existence – but then introduces a counterexample.
This is a common dialectical move: grant the premise but find a counterexample.
2
Understand the argument from divine knowledge
The opponent states that God is knowing of infinite objects. Each object requires a distinct relation (idafa). The proof of distinctness is that one can know God knows X while doubting He knows Y.
The term 'idafa' is crucial; it signifies a relational attribute.
3
Follow the conclusion and anticipated reply
From this, the opponent concludes that there are infinite relations in God's essence, contradicting the claim that infinite numbers are impossible. He then anticipates an objection: these relations have no external existence. He replies that he means only God's being knowing, which is real.
The passage cuts off during this reply. The argument is incomplete.

The Fifth Objection: Infinite Ratios of Numbers
Fifth objection: Infinite ratios are necessary and real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection which argues that infinite ratios (like half, third, etc.) are necessary and real, and how the author refutes the claim that they are mere mental considerations.

Core Idea
Infinite ratios are not just mental; they are necessary and immutable.

This passage presents the fifth objection to a previous argument. The objector claims that certain mathematical relations, such as a unit being half of two, are infinite in number and are necessarily true. The opponent might say these are only mental. The author argues that if they were only mental, they would not be necessary, but they are necessary and immutable, so they must be real.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial statement
Read the first part of the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the objector claims that every unit is half of two, third of four, etc., to infinity, and concludes that infinite ratios are actual.
The objector uses the idea of infinite progression.
2
Examine the opponent's counter-claim
The next part presents a possible objection: that ratios and relations have no external reality and are only mental considerations. Consider why someone might say this.
This is a common philosophical stance known as nominalism about relations.
3
Understand the author's refutation
Read the author's response. He argues that if ratios were merely mental, they would not be necessary; but they are necessary and immutable (e.g., a unit is always half of two). Therefore, they must be real. Reflect on the logic.
The author uses a modus tollens: if mental then not necessary; but necessary; therefore not mental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الخامس: إن كل وحدة تفرض فإنها نصف الاثنين، وثلث وربع الأربعة. وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له. فهذه النسب التي لا نهاية لها حاصلة.
لا يقال: النسب والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان، بل هي اعتبارات ذهنية لا ثبوت لها في نفس الأمر.
لأنا نقول: كل ما لا حصول له إلا بحسب فرض الذهن واعتبار العقل، فإنه لا يكون واجب الحصول في نفس الأمر. وكون الوحدة نصفاً للاثنين وثلثاً للثلاثة، وربعاً للأربعة: أمور واجبة الثبوت، لازمة التحقيق، ممتنعة التبدل والتغير. فكيف يقال: إنه لا حصول لها إلا بحسب فرض الذهن واعتبار الخيال؟
The fifth objection: Every unit that is assumed is half of two, and third and quarter of four. And so on to infinity. So these infinite ratios are actual.
It cannot be said: Ratios and relations have no existence in concrete entities, but are mental considerations with no subsistence in actual fact.
We say: Everything that has no existence except in accordance with mental assumption and intellectual consideration, then it is not necessarily obtaining in actual fact. But the fact that the unit is half of two, and third of three, and quarter of four: these are matters of necessary subsistence, requisite realization, impossible of alteration and change. So how can it be said that they have no existence except in accordance with mental assumption and imaginative consideration?
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, which argues that certain relations (like a unit being half of two) are infinite in number and are necessarily true, not mere mental fictions. The opponent might claim that relations have no external reality. The author refutes this by noting that if something is only mental, it is not necessary; but these relations are necessary, immutable, and real. Therefore, they exist in actual fact, supporting the existence of infinite numbers.

Key Terms

إضافات
اعتبارات ذهنية
واجب الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mathematical relations like 'half of two' are merely subjective mental constructions.
Correction: The author argues that such relations are necessary, immutable, and hence real, not merely mental.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that ratios are mere mental considerations?
A) Mental things can be infinite as well.
B) Ratios are necessary and immutable, so they must exist in actual fact.
C) The opponent's claim is based on imagination, not reason.
D) Ratios are only in the mind but still useful for mathematics.

Key Takeaway
The necessity and immutability of ratios like 'half of two' proves they are not mere mental fictions but real relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that the oneness of God is a necessary truth, do you live as if it were as concrete and immutable as the ratio 'half of two'? Or do you treat it as a mental notion that doesn't change your daily actions?
?

?
Consider something you believe is 'just a mental concept'—like justice or mercy. Does your belief in their reality affect your behavior? Should it, given that the author argues that necessary truths are not merely mental?

Sixth Objection: Greater and Lesser Infinities
Sixth objection: Doubling one number infinitely yields a smaller infinity than another, showing degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sixth objection uses the comparison of infinities to argue that infinities can have degrees, challenging the notion that infinity is uniform.

Core Idea
Not all infinities are equal; some are greater than others.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the idea that infinity cannot have degrees. By comparing two infinite doublings, the author shows that one infinity can be less than another. The opponent's attempt to dismiss numbers as mental constructs is refuted by the necessary and immutable ordering of numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the scenario: doubling a thousand infinitely vs. doubling half of two thousand infinitely. Notice the claim that one is less than the other, implying increase and decrease alongside infinity.
2
Identify the conclusion
The objection concludes that infinity can have degrees (increase and decrease). This contradicts the idea that infinity is absolute.
3
Consider the counterargument
The opponent says numbers are mental constructs with no real existence. This would undermine the objection.
4
Examine the rebuttal
The author asserts that the ordering of numbers is necessary and unchangeable, not arbitrary. For example, two constitutes three. This shows the ranks are real.
Notice how the author uses the terms 'تقويم' and 'تقوم' to describe the constitutive relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض السادس: إن تضعيف الألف مراراً لا نهاية لها أقل من تضعيف نصف الألفين مراراً لا نهاية لها. فههنا قد حصلت الزيادة والنقصان مع عدم النهاية.
لا يقال: إن مراتب الأعداد لا وجود لها البتة، بل هي من الاعتبارات الذهنية.
لأنا نقول: إن هذه المراتب مترتبة في أنفسها ترتباً واجب التقرر، ممتنع التغير. فالاثنان مقومان للثلاثة، والثلاثة مقومة بالاثنين. وهذا التقويم والتقوم ممتنع التغير والتبدل، فكيف يقال: هذه المراتب أمور فرضية اعتبارية؟
The sixth objection: Doubling a thousand an infinite number of times is less than doubling half of two thousand an infinite number of times. So here increase and decrease have occurred along with infinity.
It cannot be said: The ranks of numbers have no existence at all, but are among mental considerations.
We say: These ranks are ordered in themselves with an order necessary of determination, impossible of change. So two constitutes three, and three is constituted by two. And this constituting and being constituted is impossible of alteration and change. So how can it be said that these ranks are hypothetical, mental matters?
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection, which demonstrates that infinities can be compared: multiplying a thousand infinitely yields a smaller infinity than multiplying half of two thousand infinitely. This shows that some infinities are greater than others, contradicting the idea that infinity cannot have degrees. The opponent might reply that numbers are mental. The author counters that the ordering of numbers is necessary and immutable, not arbitrary mental constructs. Therefore, the existence of such infinite degrees is real.

Key Terms
تضعيف
مراتب الأعداد
زيادة ونقصان
تقويم وتقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All infinities are equal.
Correction: The objection shows that some infinities can be greater than others, implying degrees within infinity.

Checkpoint
What does the sixth objection aim to show about infinity?
A) Infinity cannot be increased or decreased.
B) Some infinities are larger than others.
C) Infinity is only a mental concept.
D) Numbers are not real.

Key Takeaway
The sixth objection demonstrates that infinities can be compared, showing that some are greater than others, which challenges the notion that infinity is without degrees.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the order of numbers is necessary and unchangeable, what does that tell you about the Creator's wisdom in establishing such a fixed reality? How does this affect your trust in the consistency of the universe and His promises?
?
Consider that something as abstract as numbers has a real, unchangeable order. How does that challenge any tendency you might have to treat moral or spiritual truths as relative or merely conventional? Will you now look for the fixed realities behind them?
?
The author insists that numerical ranks are not just mental constructs. When you think about your own life, do you treat the truths of your faith (like the oneness of God, the obligation of prayer) as fixed realities or as flexible ideas? What step will you take today to align your actions with these unchanging truths?
